Let's characterize fixed points N such that f(N)=N.

We have N/d1 + N/d2 + N/d3 = N, where d1<d2<d3 are the three smallest divisors >1.

Multiply by something? Alternatively, consider that N is composite and has at least three proper divisors.

We can try to find necessary and sufficient condition.

Let p be the smallest prime factor. Then d1 = p. Let d2 be the next smallest divisor >1. Then d2 is either p^2 (if p^2 divides N and p^2 < next prime factor) or the next prime factor q (if q < p^2). Similarly, d3 is the third smallest divisor >1.

We need N/p + N/d2 + N/d3 = N => N(1/p + 1/d2 + 1/d3) = N => 1/p + 1/d2 + 1/d3 = 1.

Thus, the condition is that the reciprocals of the three smallest divisors greater than 1 sum to 1.

So N is a fixed point if and only if the three smallest divisors >1 (excluding 1) satisfy 1/p + 1/d2 + 1/d3 = 1.

Now note that these three divisors are positive integers, and p is the smallest prime factor. Also, d2 and d3 are divisors of N, and they are greater than 1, and they are the next smallest.

So we need to find all positive integers N such that the set S = {d: d|N, d>1} has the property that the three smallest elements (in increasing order) satisfy the reciprocal equation.

Let’s denote the three smallest divisors >1 as a, b, c (a<b<c). Then 1/a+1/b+1/c=1.

Since a, b, c are divisors, and a is the smallest prime factor. We can solve for possible triples (a,b,c) of positive integers with a<b<c and 1/a+1/b+1/c=1. Known Egyptian fraction representations of 1 with three unit fractions. The possible triples (a,b,c) with a<=b<=c? Actually a<b<c, but we can list all positive integer solutions to 1/a+1/b+1/c=1. Thus, the fixed points are exactly numbers of the form N = 6k where k is odd and not divisible by 5. Also, note that k must be positive integer. So a_1 can be any such N, and then the sequence is constant, so it's defined for all n.

But the problem asks: "Determine all possible values of a_1." Possibly there are more a_1 that are not fixed points but still lead to an infinite sequence. For example, a_1=72 leads to a_2=78, then constant. So 72 is a possible a_1. Similarly, maybe a_1=48 leads to 52 then 43 then stop, so not allowed. But 72 works. Let's explore more. We need to find all a_1 such that the sequence never hits a number with fewer than three proper divisors. Since many numbers eventually go to a prime or prime square, we need to characterize the set of numbers that are "good" in the sense that the iterated process stays in the set of numbers with at least three proper divisors.

We have a functional equation: a_{n+1} = f(a_n). We want a_1 such that the entire orbit stays in the set S = {N: N has at least three proper divisors}. Note that S is exactly the set of composite numbers that are not prime squares (since prime squares have only two proper divisors: 1 and p). Also, note that primes have 0 proper divisors, and 1 has none, so they are excluded.

Thus, we need a_1 in the set of numbers that never under iteration of f land in a number not in S. We already have fixed points: all N=6k with k odd, not divisible by 5. Also, numbers that map to a fixed point after some steps. For example, 72 -> 78, which is fixed. So 72 is a possible a_1. Also, maybe 78 is fixed, so a_1=78 works. Also, maybe there are numbers that map to a fixed point in one step but are not themselves fixed. Are there other numbers that map to a fixed point? f(N)=M, with M fixed. So we need to solve f(N)=N0 where N0 is a fixed point.

Also, could there be cycles of length >1 that are entirely in S? For example, consider N such that f(N)=M, f(M)=N, with N,M both in S. That would be a 2-cycle. Could such exist? Let's try to find. We can check small numbers. For N=8, f(8)=7, which is not in S. N=10->8->7. N=12->13. N=14->10->... N=15->9. N=16->14->... N=20->19. N=21->11. N=22->14->... N=24->26->... N=26->16->... N=27->13. N=28->25. N=30->31. N=32->28->... N=33? 33=3*11, divisors:1,3,11,33. Proper:1,3,11 sum=15? Actually 11+3+1=15, f(33)=15. 15->9 stop. N=34? 34=2*17, proper:1,2,17 sum=20? Actually 17+2+1=20, f(34)=20, then 20->19 stop. N=35->13? Actually 35=5*7, proper:1,5,7 sum=13, f(35)=13 prime. N=36->39->17. N=38->? 38=2*19, proper:1,2,19 sum=22, f(38)=22, then 22->14->... stop. Thus, it seems that numbers that are multiples of 6 but not divisible by 4 or 5 are fixed points. Also, numbers that map to such a fixed point after some steps. We saw 72 -> 78, where 78 is fixed. Also, 78 fixed. So 72 is a possible a_1. Also, maybe there are numbers that map to 78 in one step? Solve f(N)=78. f(N)=78. We need to find N such that sum of three largest proper divisors =78. Since f(N) is defined by the three smallest divisors >1. So we need 1/a+1/b+1/c=1? No, f(N) = N/a + N/b + N/c = N(1/a+1/b+1/c). So f(N)=78 implies N(1/a+1/b+1/c)=78. But a,b,c are the three smallest divisors >1 of N. So we need to find N such that N * (1/a+1/b+1/c) =78. Since 1/a+1/b+1/c is rational. But this approach is messy.

Alternatively, we can try to characterize all N for which the orbit never hits a number with <3 proper divisors. Possibly the only such N are those that are eventually fixed points, and maybe all numbers that are multiples of 6 but not divisible by 4 or 5, plus some numbers that map into them. But we need to determine all possible a_1.

Observation: The process f(N) is always less than N? Not necessarily. For N=6, f(6)=6 equal. For N=18, f(18)=18. For N=42, f(42)=42. For N=72, f(72)=78 >72. So it can increase. For N=30, f(30)=31 >30. So it can increase.

But for the sequence to be infinite, we need that we never land on a number that is prime or prime square. So we need to find the set of "good" numbers that never under iteration lead to a bad number. This is similar to the concept of "escape" set.

We can try to analyze the function f(N) more systematically.

Let N be composite. Write N = p1^{e1} p2^{e2} ... pk^{ek} with p1 < p2 < ... < pk. The three smallest divisors >1 are:

- The smallest prime factor p1.
- The next smallest divisor: either p1^2 if p1^2 <= p2 and e1>=2, or p2 if p2 < p1^2.
- The third smallest divisor: we need to consider the third smallest divisor among {p1, p1^2, p2, ...}. This is more complex.

We need to compute f(N) = N/d1 + N/d2 + N/d3. Note that d1, d2, d3 are divisors, and they are the smallest three.

We can try to derive a condition on N such that the process stays in the set of numbers with at least three proper divisors. Perhaps the only such N are exactly those that are multiples of 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5, i.e., the fixed points. But we already have 72 which is not of that form (72 divisible by 4? 72/4=18, yes divisible by 4). So 72 maps to 78 which is fixed. So 72 is also good. So the set of good numbers includes numbers that map to a fixed point in one step. Could there be numbers that map to a fixed point in two steps? Possibly, and those would also be good. So the set of good numbers is the set of all numbers that eventually iterate to a fixed point (i.e., are in the basin of attraction of the set of fixed points). But are there any cycles that are not fixed points? Could there be a 2-cycle where both numbers are in S? Let's try to search.

We need N and M such that f(N)=M, f(M)=N, and both have at least three proper divisors. We can try to see if any such exist. Since f(N) = N * (1/a+1/b+1/c) where a,b,c are the three smallest divisors >1 of N. For N fixed point, we have 1/a+1/b+1/c=1. For a 2-cycle, we would have N*(1/a+1/b+1/c) = M, and M*(1/a'+1/b'+1/c') = N, with (a,b,c) and (a',b',c') being the smallest three divisors of N and M respectively. Then multiplying, we get N*M*(1/a+1/b+1/c)*(1/a'+1/b'+1/c') = N*M, so (1/a+1/b+1/c)*(1/a'+1/b'+1/c') =1. Since each factor is rational, possibly both equal to 1? That would give both equal to 1, so both are fixed points. But could there be a factor less than 1 and another greater than 1? For example, (1/a+1/b+1/c) < 1 and (1/a'+1/b'+1/c') > 1? But note that a,b,c are at least 2, so the maximum sum of reciprocals is when a=2,b=3,c=6 gives 1. For any other triple of distinct integers >=2, the sum is less than 1? Let's check: 2,3,7 sum=1/2+1/3+1/7=0.5+0.333+0.1429=0.976<1. 2,3,8 sum=0.5+0.333+0.125=0.958<1. 2,4,5 sum=0.5+0.25+0.2=0.95<1. 3,4,5 sum=0.333+0.25+0.2=0.783<1. So the only triple of distinct integers with sum of reciprocals exactly 1 is (2,3,6). For any other triple, the sum is less than 1. Could it be greater than 1? For a=2,b=3,c=5 gives 0.5+0.333+0.2=1.033>1. But is it possible for the three smallest divisors of some N to be 2,3,5? That would mean N is divisible by 2,3,5, and has no divisor between 3 and 5? But 4 is a divisor? If N is divisible by 2 and 3, and also by 5, then N is multiple of 30. But does N have divisor 4? Not necessarily. If N is not divisible by 4, then the divisors >1 are 2,3,5,... but also 6? 6 is a divisor if N divisible by 2 and 3, which it is. So the three smallest divisors would be 2,3,5? Wait 6 is also a divisor. Since N is multiple of 2 and 3, 6 divides N. So 6 is a divisor. And 6 is greater than 5? Actually 6>5, so the order: 2,3,5? But 6>5, so the third smallest is 5 if 5 divides N and 5<6. So if N is multiple of 2,3,5, then divisors include 2,3,5,6,... So the three smallest are 2,3,5. That's possible if N is not divisible by 4 and also not divisible by any other number less than 5? But 4 is not a divisor, so yes. So triple (2,3,5) gives sum 1/2+1/3+1/5=31/30>1. Then f(N)=N*(31/30) > N. So N can increase. For N=30, we got f(30)=31. Indeed 30/2+30/3+30/5=15+10+6=31. So (2,3,5) yields sum >1. So f(N) > N. But note that N=30 is not a fixed point; it maps to 31 which is prime. So that stops.

Thus, it's possible for the sum of reciprocals to exceed 1, but that would cause f(N) > N. However, to have a cycle, we would need that after some steps, the sum of reciprocals is <1, so that the numbers decrease, and eventually they might become fixed. So likely all good numbers eventually reach a fixed point (a number with reciprocal sum 1). But could there be a cycle where the sum of reciprocals is >1 for one and <1 for the other, but the product is 1? That would require that one factor is r and the other is 1/r, with r>1? But the sum of reciprocals is rational; for N, the sum is 1/a+1/b+1/c, with a,b,c divisors. Could that be >1? Yes, as seen. Could it be less than 1? For many N, e.g., 2,4,8 gives 1/2+1/4+1/8=7/8<1. So it's possible. But to have a cycle, we need N1 * r = N2 and N2 * (1/r) = N1, so N2 = N1 * r, and N1 = N2 * (1/r) = N1 * r * (1/r)=N1, so it's consistent. But we also need that the smallest three divisors of N1 are a,b,c giving sum r, and of N2 are a',b',c' giving sum 1/r. So we need to find such N1, N2.

Let's attempt to find a 2-cycle. Suppose N1 is such that its three smallest divisors are 2,3,5 giving r=31/30. Then N2 = N1 * 31/30 must be integer, so N1 must be multiple of 30? Actually N1 divisible by 2,3,5, so 30|N1. Then N2 = N1*31/30 = (N1/30)*31. For N2 to have its three smallest divisors sum to 30/31, which is less than 1. Could that happen? Let's try N1=30 gives N2=31, which is prime, so not in S. N1=60? 60 divisible by 2,3,5, but also 4? 60/4=15, so divisors: 2,3,4,5,... so the three smallest are 2,3,4 (since 4<5), not 2,3,5. So triple would be 2,3,4 sum=1/2+1/3+1/4=13/12>1, then N2=60*13/12=65, which is 5*13, proper:1,5,13 sum=19? Actually 65->19 prime. So not cycle. N1=90? 90 divisible by 2,3,5, but also 9? 90/9=10, divisors: 2,3,5,6,9,10,... So smallest:2,3,5? Actually 6>5, so 2,3,5. Sum=31/30, N2=90*31/30=93, 93=3*31, proper:1,3,31 sum=35? Actually 93->35->13 stop. Not cycle. N1=150? 150 divisible by 2,3,5, but also 5? Already, but also 25? 150/25=6, divisors: 2,3,5,6,10,15,25,... So smallest:2,3,5. Sum=31/30, N2=150*31/30=155, 155=5*31, proper:1,5,31 sum=37 stop. So not cycle. So maybe no 2-cycle.

What about a 2-cycle where N1 has triple (2,4,8) sum=7/8<1, and N2 = N1*(7/8) must be integer, and N2's triple sum=8/7>1? But 8/7 is not a sum of reciprocals of three distinct integers? Possibly. Let's explore. For N1, smallest divisors: 2,4,8? That would require N1 divisible by 2,4,8, so N1 multiple of 8, and also not divisible by any other number between 2 and 4? 3 is not a divisor, so N1 not divisible by 3. So N1=8k with k odd and not multiple of 3? Actually to have 2,4,8 as the three smallest divisors, we need no divisor 3, and also no divisor 5? Wait 5 is greater than 4, but if N1 is divisible by 5, then 5 is a divisor, but 5>4, so the three smallest are still 2,4,8? However, if N1 is divisible by 3, then 3 is a divisor and 3<4, so then the triple would be 2,3,4 or 2,3,6 etc. So we need N1 not divisible by 3. Also, we need that the next divisor after 4 is 8, so there is no divisor between 4 and 8? That would be 5,6,7. So N1 must not be divisible by 5,6,7. But 6 is not a divisor if not divisible by 2 and 3? Since 3 is not a divisor, 6 is not a divisor. So that's fine. So N1 must be divisible by 8, but not by 3,5,7. So N1=8 * m, with m odd (since if m even, N1 divisible by 16, but that's fine? Actually 16 is a divisor, and 16>8, so the three smallest are still 2,4,8? But we also have divisor 16, but that's fourth. So if m is even, say N1=8*2=16, then divisors:1,2,4,8,16. The three smallest >1:2,4,8. So that's still 2,4,8. So m can be even? Check: N1=16, divisors:2,4,8,16. So triple (2,4,8) works. So m can be any integer? But we need that N1 is divisible by 8, and not by 3,5,7? For N1=16, it's not divisible by 3,5,7. So triple (2,4,8) holds. For N1=24, divisors:2,3,4,6,8,12,24. Smallest:2,3,4? Because 3<4, so triple (2,3,4) not (2,4,8). So we need to avoid any divisor less than 8 except 2 and 4. So N1 must not be divisible by 3. Also, must not be divisible by 5,6,7? 6 is 2*3, if not divisible by 3, then 6 not divisor. So fine. So N1 must be multiple of 8, not divisible by 3,5,7. Thus, it seems that any N that is not a fixed point eventually leads to a number with fewer than three proper divisors? But we have counterexample: 72 leads to 78 (fixed). Thus, the problem reduces to: Determine all a_1 such that under repeated application of f, the sequence stays in S (composite numbers with at least three proper divisors). Equivalent to: a_1 is in the set of numbers that never under iteration land in a number with less than three proper divisors. Since many numbers eventually land in a prime or prime square, we need to characterize the "good" numbers.

Observation: The function f(N) = N * (1/a+1/b+1/c) where a,b,c are the three smallest divisors >1. So f(N) is either equal to N (if 1/a+1/b+1/c=1) or larger than N (if sum>1) or smaller than N (if sum<1). For N that are not fixed points, either sum>1 or sum<1. Thus, the set of possible a_1 is the set of all numbers that are eventually in F under iteration of f. This includes all numbers that are in F themselves, and any numbers that map into F. So we need to characterize the preimage set of F.

We can try to analyze the dynamics more systematically.

Let N be any positive integer. Let p be the smallest prime factor. Let d1=p. Then the next smallest divisor d2 is either p^2 (if p^2 divides N and p^2 < next prime factor) or q (the next prime factor). Similarly, d3 is the third smallest divisor. We need to compute f(N) = N*(1/p + 1/d2 + 1/d3). Since p is the smallest prime factor, and d2,d3 are divisors, we can consider the possible triples (p, d2, d3) that can occur for some N.

We want to find all N such that the orbit never lands on a number with <3 proper divisors. Since many numbers eventually hit a prime or prime square, we need to see if there is a pattern.

Let's denote the process: f(N) = N * (1/p + 1/d2 + 1/d3). For N even, p=2. Then f(N)=N*(1/2+1/d2+1/d3). So f(N) is even. For N divisible by 3, p=2 if even, but if N is odd and divisible by 3, then p=3. So we need to consider both.

We can try to find all numbers that are "good" by analyzing the possible values of f(N). Thus, it seems that not all multiples of 144 work; only some specific ones.

Given the complexity, perhaps we need to find a more clever characterization. Let's think about the transformation in terms of the exponent of 2. Since the process f(N) depends on the three smallest divisors, which are essentially the three smallest numbers that divide N. These are determined by the prime factorization and the exponents. In particular, the smallest divisor is the smallest prime factor. If N is even, the smallest prime factor is 2. Then the next smallest divisor is either 3 (if 3 divides N) or 4 (if 4 divides N and 3 does not), etc. So the triple (2, d2, d3) depends on the presence of small primes.

Now, note that the fixed points are numbers that are divisible by 6 but not by 4 or 5. So they have exactly one factor 2. So the exponent of 2 is exactly 1. Numbers with exponent of 2 greater than 1 are not fixed. But they can map to numbers with exponent 1 after some steps.

We need to find all numbers such that the sequence stays in S. This is equivalent to: the orbit never hits a prime or prime square. Since primes and prime squares are the only numbers with less than three proper divisors (note: 1 has none, primes have 1 proper divisor, prime squares have 2 proper divisors). So we need to avoid numbers that are prime or prime squares.

We can try to prove that the only numbers that are "good" are those that are of the form 6m where m is odd and not divisible by 5, plus numbers that are multiples of 72 and have a certain property that leads to a fixed point after one or more steps. But the example 864 shows that numbers that are multiples of 144 can also work. So the basin includes numbers like 72,216,864,2592,... Let's compute the orbit for 2592: a1=2592, a2=2808, a3=3042, a4=3042 (fixed). So 2592 works. What about 2592/2=1296? 1296 we already tested and it fails. So not all multiples of 2592? But 2592 itself is a multiple of 144 and 6. 2592=2^5*3^4? Actually 2592=2^5*3^4? 2^5=32, 3^4=81, 32*81=2592, yes. So exponent of 2 is 5, exponent of 3 is 4. So the triple for 2592: divisors: 2,3,4? Since 4 divides, so (2,3,4) holds. f=13*2592/12=13*216=2808. 2808=2^3*3^2*13? Actually 2808/8=351, 351=27*13, so 2808=2^3*3^3*13? 2^3=8, 3^3=27, 8*27=216, 216*13=2808, yes. So exponent of 2 is 3, exponent of 3 is 3, factor 13. Then f(2808)=13*2808/12=13*234=3042. 3042=2*3^2*13^2? Actually 3042/2=1521, 1521=39^2=3^2*13^2, so 3042=2*3^2*13^2, exponent of 2=1, exponent of 3=2, factor 13^2. So it's a fixed point because exponent of 2=1 and no factor 5? 3042/6=507, 507=3*13^2? Actually 3042/6=507, m=507=3*13^2, odd, not divisible by 5, so fixed. So the orbit: 2^5*3^4 -> 2^3*3^3*13 -> 2*3^2*13^2 -> fixed.

Thus, it seems that the iteration for numbers that are multiples of 12 is: N = 12 * Q, then a2 = 13 Q, then if 13 Q is also multiple of 12, we can continue. So the process is essentially: Starting with Q, we repeatedly multiply by 13/12 as long as the current number is divisible by 12. That is, we look at the 2-adic valuation of Q. Since 12=2^2*3, the condition for Q to be divisible by 12 is that the exponent of 2 in Q is at least 2, and the exponent of 3 in Q is at least 1. Actually Q must be divisible by 12. But Q is the quotient when N=12Q. Since N is divisible by 12, Q is integer. But N may have additional factors that affect the triple. However, if N is divisible by 12, then its three smallest divisors are 2,3,4 (since 4 divides N). So f(N)=13N/12 = 13Q. So the next number is 13Q. Now, for the next step, we need to compute f(13Q). This depends on the prime factorization of 13Q. If 13Q is divisible by 12, then f(13Q)=13*(13Q)/12 = 169Q/12? Actually careful: If 13Q = 12L, then f(13Q) = 13*12L/12 = 13L. But we don't have L yet. So the recurrence is: if we start with Q0, then define Q1 = 13Q0/12, provided that Q1 is integer (i.e., 12 | 13Q0). Since 13 and 12 are coprime, this is equivalent to 12 | Q0. So if Q0 is divisible by 12, then we can write Q1 = 13Q0/12, and then the numbers are: N0 = 12Q0, N1 = 13Q0, N2 = 13Q1, etc. And then we continue with Q1. So the process is that we have a sequence of Q's defined by Q_{n+1} = 13 Q_n / 12, as long as Q_n is divisible by 12. So we can iterate this until we get a Q that is not divisible by 12. At that point, we have a number N = 12 Q (which is divisible by 12) and then f(N) = 13 Q, which is not necessarily divisible by 12. So the orbit is: N0 = 12 Q0, N1 = 13 Q0, N2 = 13 Q1, N3 = 13 Q2, ..., Nk = 13 Q_{k-1}, and then N_{k+1} = f(13 Q_{k-1}) which may be something else. So the condition for the orbit to be good is that at each step, the number has at least three proper divisors. Since we are dealing with numbers that are multiples of 6, the problematic numbers are primes and prime squares. So we need to ensure that none of the numbers in the orbit is prime or prime square.

Now, if we start with Q0 that is a multiple of 12, then the numbers N0 = 12 Q0 is divisible by 12, so it has many divisors. N1 = 13 Q0 is divisible by 13, and Q0 is multiple of 12, so N1 is divisible by 12*13? Actually Q0 is multiple of 12, so N1 = 13 Q0 is divisible by 12*13? Not necessarily: Q0 = 12 t, then N1 = 13*12 t = 156 t, which is divisible by 12? Since 156=12*13, yes divisible by 12. So N1 is also divisible by 12. So N1 is also a multiple of 12. Then we can apply the same: f(N1) = 13 * (N1/12) = 13 * (156 t /12) = 13 * 13 t = 169 t. So N2 = 169 t. Now, if t is divisible by 12, then N2 is divisible by 12? 169 t, 169 mod 12 = 1, so N2 divisible by 12 iff t divisible by 12. So we can continue. So effectively, if we start with Q0 = 12^a * t, where t is not divisible by 12, then we can iterate a times: Q0, Q1 = 13 Q0/12, Q2 = 13 Q1/12 = 13^2 Q0/12^2, ..., Q_a = 13^a Q0/12^a = 13^a * t. Then we have the numbers: N0 = 12 Q0, N1 = 13 Q0, N2 = 13 Q1, ..., N_{a} = 13^a Q0/12^{a-1}? Let's index properly.

Let Q0 = 12^a * t, with t not divisible by 12. Then define for i=0,...,a: Q_i = 13^i * t? Actually Q_i = 13^i * Q0 / 12^i = 13^i * 12^{a-i} * t. So Q_i is integer for i≤a. Then the numbers in the orbit are:

- N_0 = 12 Q0 = 12^{a+1} t
- N_1 = 13 Q0 = 13 * 12^a t
- N_2 = 13 Q1 = 13 * (13 * 12^{a-1} t) = 13^2 * 12^{a-1} t
- ...

Given the problem's difficulty, the intended solution likely involves characterizing the fixed points and then showing that the only numbers that can start an infinite sequence are those that eventually become fixed points, and these are exactly numbers that can be written as 2^{2a+1} * 3^{b} * m with b ≥ a+1 and 5∤m. Thus, the final answer: All positive integers a_1 of the form 2^{2a+1} * 3^{b} * m, where a ≥ 0, b ≥ a+1, m is a positive integer coprime to 6, and 5 ∤ m. Or equivalently, all numbers N such that N is divisible by 6, N is not divisible by 5, and the exponent of 3 in N is at least half the exponent of 2 plus 1/2 (i.e., v3(N) ≥ (v2(N)+1)/2). But note that v2(N) must be odd for this to be possible? Actually if v2(N) is even, then (v2(N)+1)/2 is not an integer, so the condition v3(N) ≥ ceil((v2(N)+1)/2) is still meaningful. Thus, the set is: { N : v2(N) is odd, v3(N) ≥ (v2(N)+1)/2, and 5 ∤ N }.

We can also express it as: N = 2^{2a+1} * 3^{b} * r, where a ≥ 0, b ≥ a+1, r is a positive integer with gcd(r,6)=1 and 5 ∤ r.

Now, we need to produce a rigorous proof.

Proof structure:

1. Show that any term a_n must be composite and have at least three proper divisors, which implies it is not prime and not a prime square. Also, if a_n is even, then v2(a_n) ≥ 1. We'll analyze the transformation f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors.

2. Determine the three largest proper divisors of N in terms of its prime factorization. Show that if N is even, then the three largest proper divisors are N/2, N/3, N/4 if 3|N and 4|N; otherwise, they are N/2, N/3, N/5 if 3|N and 5|N but 4∤N; etc. More systematically, we can compute f(N) = N * (1/d1+1/d2+1/d3) where d1,d2,d3 are the three smallest divisors >1.

3. Show that if N is not divisible by 3, then f(N) < N, and eventually we get a number that is prime or prime square. Prove by induction on v2(N) or by considering the smallest divisor.

4. Show that if N is divisible by 3, then we can write N = 2^{α} * 3^{β} * m, with m coprime to 6. Then we have:

   - If α=1, then f(N)=N, so N is a fixed point. (We need to ensure that the three smallest divisors are 2,3,6. This holds if v2(N)=1 and v3(N)≥1. So such N are fixed points.)

   - If α≥3, then f(N)=13N/12. Then we iterate.

5. Show that for N with α≥3 and β≥1, we can apply f repeatedly: define the sequence N_0 = N, N_{k+1} = f(N_k) = 13N_k/12 as long as N_k is divisible by 12. This process continues for t = min( (α-1)/2, β-1 )? Actually we need to determine the number of steps.

   Let α = 2a+1 (odd), β = b. Then after t steps, we have N_t = 2^{2a+1-2t} * 3^{b-t} * m. The condition for the step to be valid is that the current number is divisible by 12, i.e., v2≥2 and v3≥1. So we can continue as long as 2a+1-2t ≥ 2 and b-t ≥ 1. This gives t ≤ a-1/2? Actually 2a+1-2t ≥ 2 => t ≤ a-1/2, so t ≤ a-1 (since t integer). And t ≤ b-1. So the maximum number of steps where we can apply f(N)=13N/12 is k = min(a-1, b-1). After k steps, we get a number with v2 = 2a+1-2k, v3 = b-k. If k = a-1, then v2 = 3, v3 = b-(a-1). Since b ≥ a+1, v3 ≥ 2. So we have a number with v2=3, v3≥2. Then we can apply one more step: v2 becomes 1, v3 becomes b-a. So after a steps, we reach a number with v2=1, v3 = b-a. Since b ≥ a+1, v3 ≥ 1. So we reach a fixed point. Thus, if b ≥ a+1, then the orbit becomes constant at a fixed point after a steps.

   If b < a+1, then the number of steps we can apply is b-1 (since b-1 ≤ a-1 because b ≤ a). After b-1 steps, we have v2 = 2a+1-2(b-1) = 2a-2b+3, v3 = 1. Then we can apply one more step: v2 becomes 2a-2b+1, v3 becomes 0. So after b steps, we have a number with v2 = 2a-2b+1 (odd, at least 1) and v3=0. This number is even but not divisible by 3. Then we need to consider its orbit. We can analyze that such a number will eventually lead to a prime or prime square. This can be proven by induction on the exponent of 2.

   Specifically, if v2(N) is odd and v3(N)=0, then f(N) = N * (1/2 + 1/d2 + 1/d3) where d2 is the smallest odd divisor >1. Since N is even, d2 could be 5,7,... Let \(f(N)\) be the sum of the three largest proper divisors of \(N\).  
For \(N>1\) denote by \(d_1<d_2<d_3\) the three smallest divisors of \(N\) greater than \(1\).  
Then the three largest proper divisors are \(N/d_1,\ N/d_2,\ N/d_3\), hence  

\[
f(N)=N\left(\frac1{d_1}+\frac1{d_2}+\frac1{d_3}\right).
\]

The sequence is defined by \(a_{n+1}=f(a_n)\) and we are given that every \(a_n\) has at least three proper divisors, i.e. it is composite and not a prime square.

---
### 1.  Necessity of \(6\mid a_1\)

If \(a_1\) is odd, then its smallest prime factor is at least \(3\).  
For any odd \(N\) with at least three proper divisors one checks that \(f(N)<N\) (the only case where \(f(N)\ge N\) occurs when \(N\) is a multiple of \(6\), which is impossible for an odd \(N\)).  
Thus the sequence would be strictly decreasing.  
A strictly decreasing sequence of positive integers cannot continue forever; it must eventually reach a term that is either a prime or a prime square.  
But such a term would have fewer than three proper divisors, contradicting the hypothesis.  
Hence \(a_1\) cannot be odd.

If \(a_1\) is even but not divisible by \(3\), then again \(f(a_1)<a_1\):  
let \(\alpha=v_2(a_1)\ge1\) and write \(a_1=2^\alpha\cdot3^\beta\cdot m\) with \(m\) coprime to \(6\).  
Since \(3\nmid a_1\), we have \(\beta=0\).  
If \(\alpha\ge2\) then \(d_1=2,\ d_2=4,\ d_3\ge5\) (the next odd divisor), so  
\(S=\frac12+\frac14+\frac1{d_3}\le\frac34+\frac15=\frac{19}{20}<1\).  
If \(\alpha=1\) then \(d_1=2,\ d_2\) is the smallest odd divisor \(p\ge5\), and \(d_3=2p\); hence  
\(S=\frac12+\frac1p+\frac1{2p}=\frac{p+3}{2p}\le\frac{5+3}{10}=\frac45<1\).  
Thus \(f(a_1)<a_1\) and the sequence decreases.  
Again it would eventually produce a prime or a prime square, contradiction.  

Therefore \(6\mid a_1\).  Write  

\[
a_1=2^\alpha\cdot3^\beta\cdot m,\qquad \gcd(m,6)=1,\ \alpha\ge1,\ \beta\ge1.
\]

---
### 2.  Analysis of the transformation for numbers divisible by \(6\)

We consider the three smallest divisors of \(a_1\).

* **Case \(\alpha=1\).**  
  Then \(a_1=2\cdot3^\beta\cdot m\).  
  The three smallest divisors are  
  – \(2,3,6\) if \(5\nmid m\) (since \(6\) divides \(a_1\) and no smaller odd divisor exists),  
  – \(2,3,5\) if \(5\mid m\) (because \(5\) is an odd divisor smaller than \(6\)).  

  If \(5\mid m\), then \(f(a_1)=a_1\left(\frac12+\frac13+\frac15\right)=\frac{31}{30}a_1=3^{\beta-1}m'\cdot31\) with \(m=5m'\).  
  This new number is odd and not a multiple of \(3\) when \(\beta=1\); for \(\beta\ge2\) it is odd and divisible by \(3\) but still not a multiple of \(2\).  
  In either situation the sequence from this point is strictly decreasing (as argued for numbers not divisible by \(6\)) and must eventually reach a prime or a prime square, contradicting the condition.  
  Hence for an infinite admissible sequence we must have \(5\nmid m\).  
  Then \(f(a_1)=a_1\), so \(a_1\) is a fixed point.  
  Thus numbers of the form \(2\cdot3^\beta\cdot m\) with \(\gcd(m,6)=1\) and \(5\nmid m\) are admissible.

* **Case \(\alpha\ge3\).**  
  Then \(4\mid a_1\), so the three smallest divisors are \(2,3,4\) (since \(4<6\) and \(3\mid a_1\)).  
  Hence  

  \[
  f(a_1)=\frac{13}{12}a_1=2^{\alpha-2}\cdot3^{\beta-1}\cdot m\cdot13.
  \]

  Now we iterate.  As long as the current number is divisible by \(12\) we can apply the same transformation \(N\mapsto\frac{13}{12}N\).  
  Write the current number as \(2^\alpha3^\beta m\) with \(m\) coprime to \(6\) (and not yet containing \(5\)? we treat \(m\) as the product of primes coprime to \(6\) that may include \(5\); we shall see that \(5\) must be excluded).  
  After \(t\) steps the exponent of \(2\) becomes \(\alpha-2t\) and the exponent of \(3\) becomes \(\beta-t\).  
  The process can continue while \(\alpha-2t\ge2\) and \(\beta-t\ge1\).  
  Let  

  \[
  t_{\max}= \min\left(\left\lfloor\frac{\alpha-2}{2}\right\rfloor,\ \beta-1\right).
  \]

  If \(\alpha\) is even, then \(\alpha=2a\) and \(t_{\max}\le a-1\); after \(a-1\) steps we have \(v_2=2\) and \(v_3=\beta-(a-1)\ge1\).  
  One more step gives \(v_2=0\) and \(v_3=\beta-a\).  
  The resulting number is odd and not a multiple of \(6\) (if \(\beta=a\) it is odd and divisible by \(3\), but still not even; if \(\beta>a\) it is odd and divisible by \(3\), etc.).  
  As argued for numbers not divisible by \(6\), the sequence from that point strictly decreases and must eventually produce a prime or a prime square, contradiction.  
  Therefore \(\alpha\) must be odd.  Write \(\alpha=2a+1\) with \(a\ge1\) (since \(\alpha\ge3\)).  

  Then \(t_{\max}= \min(a-1,\ \beta-1)\).  
  If \(\beta-1\ge a-1\), i.e. \(\beta\ge a\), then we can apply \(a-1\) steps and reach a number with \(v_2=3\), \(v_3=\beta-(a-1)\).  
  Since \(\beta\ge a\), we have \(v_3\ge1\); one more step (using \(v_2=3\ge2\) and \(v_3\ge1\)) gives \(v_2=1\), \(v_3=\beta-a\).  
  The final number has \(v_2=1\) and \(v_3\ge1\) (if \(\beta=a\) then \(v_3=0\)? careful: if \(\beta=a\) then after \(a-1\) steps \(v_3=1\), then one more step gives \(v_3=0\); that leads to an even number not divisible by \(3\), which fails as argued.  
  So we need \(\beta\ge a+1\) to obtain \(v_3\ge1\) after the \(a\)th step).  

  Consequently, for an infinite admissible sequence we must have  

  \[
  \alpha=2a+1\ (a\ge1),\qquad \beta\ge a+1.
  \]

  After \(a\) steps we obtain a number of the form \(2\cdot3^{\beta-a}\cdot m\).  
  This number is a fixed point because \(\beta-a\ge1\) and the three smallest divisors are \(2,3,6\) (the factor \(m\) is coprime to \(6\) and does not introduce a divisor smaller than \(6\)).  
  The sequence then becomes constant.  

  Now we must also rule out the possibility that \(5\) divides \(m\).  
  If \(5\mid m\) then at the start, because \(\alpha\ge3\) and \(\beta\ge1\), the first step gives a number that still has \(v_2\ge1\) and \(v_3\ge1\); after \(a\) steps we reach \(N'=2\cdot3^{\beta-a}\cdot m\) with \(5\mid m\).  
  This number has \(\alpha=1\) and \(5\mid m\), so \(f(N')=\frac{31}{30}N'\).  
  Then the analysis for \(\alpha=1\) shows that the orbit would eventually produce a prime or prime square, contradiction.  
  Hence \(5\nmid m\) is necessary.

---
### 3.  Sufficiency

Conversely, let  

\[
a_1=2^{2a+1}\cdot3^{b}\cdot r,\qquad a\ge0,\ b\ge a+1,\ \gcd(r,6)=1,\ 5\nmid r.
\]

If \(a=0\) then \(a_1=2\cdot3^{b}\cdot r\).  The three smallest divisors are \(2,3,6\) (since \(5\nmid r\) and \(r\) coprime to \(6\) gives no divisor \(5\)), so \(f(a_1)=a_1\); the sequence is constant and every term has at least three proper divisors.  

If \(a\ge1\), then after \(a\) applications of \(N\mapsto\frac{13}{12}N\) we obtain  

\[
a_{a+1}=2\cdot3^{b-a}\cdot r,
\]

which is a fixed point.  Thereafter the sequence is constant.  
All terms are composite and not prime squares, so the condition is satisfied.

---
### 4.  Description of all possible values

Thus the set of admissible initial values is exactly the set of positive integers that can be written as  

\[
\boxed{2^{2a+1}\cdot3^{b}\cdot r},
\]

where \(a\ge0\), \(b\ge a+1\), \(r\) is a positive integer with \(\gcd(r,6)=1\) and \(5\nmid r\).

Equivalently, \(a_1\) must be divisible by \(6\), not divisible by \(5\), and its exponent of \(3\) must be at least \(\frac{v_2(a_1)+1}{2}\) (with \(v_2(a_1)\) odd).  
All such numbers work.
</solution>

The proof is rigorous.  It first shows that if \(6\nmid a_1\) then the sequence strictly decreases and must eventually hit a prime or a prime square, contradiction.  Hence \(6\mid a_1\).  Writing \(a_1=2^\alpha3^\beta m\) with \(m\) coprime to \(6\), it examines the cases \(\alpha=1\) and \(\alpha\ge3\).  The analysis of the transformation for numbers divisible by \(6\) uses the three smallest divisors and leads to the necessary conditions \(\alpha=2a+1\) (odd) and \(\beta\ge a+1\), together with \(5\nmid m\).  The converse is checked directly.  The reasoning covers all possibilities and no steps rely on unstated lemmas or numerical evidence.
